The Phenomenon of Reversal and the Dynamic of the Archetype
late culture may symboljze an early phase, while a monument o( an
early culture may symboliz-e a late phase, of archetypal development. Similarly, in the analysis of a human individual, symbols and symptoms of future and later developments may appear at the very beginning, and conversely infantile and archaic elements may p resent themselves in stages of relati vely [uller psychic development. Our conscious classifications and schematizations are always attempts to arrive, through abstraction, at an orientat ion within the realit y of life. But this living reality, with its crests and troughs, progressions and regressions, irruptio ns and anticipations, is at every moment spontaneous and unformulablc. Even where living psychic reality follows laws, the laws are so complex and unfathomable that at best we can abstract t he most infini tesimal parL~ of them and test them by experiment in s uch a way as to be able to predict future dcvelopmenl. But in actual fact, e.g., in the reality of an analysis or of an analytical situation, and assuredly in real life, every step is individual, every drearo is unpredictable, and it is only in the most general sense that transformations can be correlated with a schema of development. In livi ng
reality, every development is surprising; with all its inner causality and purposiveness, it is new and unique for the consciousness that experiences and in,·estiga tes it, and hence defies schemafrtation. For th.is reason, the most that can be achie,·ed by an exposition in the field of depth psychology is a compromise between conscious sehemaLi?.;ilion and the unjqueness of the material that fills and overflows the schema. Thus it will always be possible to criticize the schema for hcing far from reality, and to cri ticize the material chosen to illustrate Ihe schema for being accidental. Both criticisms may be t rue to a certain <·xtcnt. B ut they will be power less to alter the fact that psychic real ity ,·,·:cdes our desi re for schematic exposition. Kor will they prev<1nt such 1111 c;position, inadequate as we know it lo be, from helping the wholt·, living man toward a fruitful orientation in the living reality of I hr

N OUR present attempt to porlrny the m·chetype of the Great Female with the help of texts 1 and reproductions, we shall follow the classification and system sketched in Part I. The dominance of the archetype ~-f the Great Mother constellates the human psyche of t he primordiaJ _sit~_Qn in which consciousness develops only slowly and only gradually emancipates itself from the domination of unconscious directing processes . _Although the_b_Mi~ni_~gs__c,f_the psychological-matriarchal llge are los t in the haze of prehistory,_j_!.s end at the dawn of ou1· h isto1·ical em unfolds magnificently before our eyes. Th_en it is replaced by the patriarchal world, and the archetype of the Great Fat her or of the Masculine, with its d ifferent symbolism, its different values, and it.5 different tendencies, becomes dominant. The stu1ly of a rchetypes involves an abundance of symbolic material from the roost diverse culLural strata and spheres; it compels tl1l' psychologist to de!ve into many different fields in which he is nol. specialized. In each of these fidds t he material is so vast that an outside,· cannot hope to master it entirely. Errors and faulty interpretation~~ particnlarly in matters of detail-are almost inevitable. Act ually the goal we have set ourselves could only be fully attained through collaboration between specialists in the science of rel igion, ethnology, and so 011 and it depth psychologist. The provisional ancl prohlematic nature ol' such a work is thcrefo1·e obvious and we can only appeal to the insiglil a nd indulgence of the reader who is more concerned with a synthesis or essentials than with t he correctness of every single detai l. But there is also a positive, compensatory factor that should not be disregarded. T lw inte,·pretation of t he sy mbolism of the unconscious in modern man-m,cl the symbolism of the unconscious remains unchanged- gives the psycho therapist an empirical-scientific foundation for the interpretation ..r collective symbolism, a foundation that must needs be lacking in cv<·r.,· · other scientist who concerns himself with this material. Our account of t he Archetypal Feminine is arranged according I " the schematic outline we have sketched in Lhe Par t I. We shall follow Ilw unfoldi,1g of the archetypal unity of Lhe Feminine from tJ1e elemrnla r.,· character through the t rans£ormativc character down to tl1c myslt-ri,..,
its po.~iLiou within I lie dtvelopment of mankind, psychic sW.S,,'\:S or hum:,n devc:lopmc11t :1t11I I Jwu he "'M :-ble in for greater ll'tehsure than he ~yml,oli:iln,
himself could re1ll?,e-to discl)ver nnd follow tJ1e
lntroductwn to Part II
of the spiritual transformation character, in which the development of feminine ,psychology reaches its culmination. This ar tificial arrangement is by no means intended to efface all the merging and crisscrossing relations. On the contrary, one of our tasks will be to account, for example, for the recurrence of the same symbols at the most diverse stages, and for tb.e transformations they undergo in these stages. While the purpose of our anangcment is to indicate the ai'chetypal structure underlying the muncrous images and symbols, it is hoped t hat our many references to cross-connections and overlappings will give an intimation of the radiant diversity of the nrchetypal reality. The ubiquity of similar symbols and symbolic conte.~ts has all too lrequently led to arbitra1·y and fantastic theories of influences, migrations, and so fort h. As a matter of principle we shall seek to avoid any such explanation by superficial "historical" relations and also t he reduction, so popular today, of similar relations to corresponding sociological phenomena. But if we content ourselves with a phenomenological picture and a psychological inte1·pretation of this picture, t his does not mean t hat we shall ma.keno attempt to work out the significant developmental trends. Yet it seems too soon to write the psychic history of mankind; the most we can offer today is certain beginnings and dirnc1.ives. And one of the necessary prelimina.ry tasks is to train tlw "eye for the archetypal." It must remain for fu ture works to exttn,i nc t he relation of particular cultures to the archet ypaJly conditioned development of consciousness and lo the dominance of parlicnlar archetypes.

Another characteristic of these figures is the disproportionate breadth and fullness of the posterior. I t would be a mistake to derive this steatom•gia from any racial characteristics.10 This quality of the Great Primordial Mother is exceedingly widespread and is found in regions where the1·e can be no q uestion of a particular African 01· Hottentot type. Here again a psychological Interpretation carries us deeper and fal'ther. It has also been repeatedly stressed thal these archetypal figures cannot be attributed to the "sexual taste" of the :male. There is no doubt that the relationship between "taste" and the figure is just the opposite and far more complex. Actually male sexuality is influenced by the archetypal figure of the Feminine, which is acLive in the unconscious. Wherever the inordinately stout, unshapely wotllan is the favored acxual object, we may infer an- unconscious-domination of the mother archetype in the male psyche." The sexual accent on the posterior mny lie derived from the fertility rites, which aim at the magical increase of animals. In the paleolithic nge, just as today among primitive peoples, there were fertility rituals In which the mating of animals, with coitus from behind, played a central role. Thus we are dealing in these rites, not with a personal cxual component in the man, but with an impersonal symbolic bc111vior, which becomes understandable in this ligh t. but rather a dynam ic lransforruation.24 The painting gives power to the living or dead obj ect, charges it n,ith mana; and in the primitive phase the red coloring that, it has been proved, was given to many prehistoric goddesses and other plastic works has the same significance. The transformative import of tattooing is also demonstrated by its use as a sign of initiation. Likewise, the painted vessel wa.~ originally sacral. It was distingu ish ed from profane vessels by its ornamcn talion,
consisting of such fundamental and meaningful symbols as the si>iral, the cr-oss, the circle, the wave.
Pl. 6 The Thracian figure of a seated goddess is one of a group of nco• lithic ceramics" that have been found in a territory extending fro ,n western Russia to the Balkans and central Eu rope. They originate in a matriarchally accented cultural milieu, )·eluted in all probability t.o Aegean culture and its precu rsors. The character of th is figure is so pl'imitive that, independently of its his torical dati ng, we must assign ii. to a very early psychological stratum. This plastic takes some of its uncanny qu ality from the contradictions contained in it. The vessel form stresses the corporeal elementary character, while the dense, tattooed ornamentation tends towarcl disembodiment; th e body is literally covered by tl1e symbol. T he tiny, barely suggested breasts reinforce th-unconscious-tendency to surpass the elementary and corporeal. Here the element of abstract io n that brings out the symbolic trnnsforma tive character of the Feminine· is pMticularly evident: the belly is not vaulted in fertility and fullncs.s: rather, the genital triangle, which is accentuated for tl1is reason, bears
the symbol of the spiral, one end roll ing upward and the other down ward.

"Abstraction" is a symbolic term for minimization oi the material clement; abstracting representation is a symbolic expression of the conflict between corpol'eal "nrl incorporeal and of a transformation lending from the corporeal to the incorporeal. An incorporeal factor operates here in a dynam ic that stands in a certain opposition t.o material reality . But in its otherness, its removal from reality, it can also appear as an abstract, a quintessential extract rrom th is real ity . This spiritual, psycl1ic clement, which in the primitive phase combines the essenti al, the symbolic, the ideationul, the transpcrsoJJal and conceptual, is the effective force in the tendency toward nhstraction. Here it is immaterial whether th is spiritual and psychic factor is \l.~perienced as something preceding or com ing afle1· the corporeal world. T he "spiritual" sou l that animates the living and departs from tlie dead belongs just as much to the region of the unworldly and preworldly as to that of t he incol'poreal and precorporcal. In so-called primitive and archaic art, especially in masks, we find nn abundance of abstrac t motifs, which possess a "spiritual" character in this primitive sense. The coexistence of naturalistic elenwnts a ncl ol nhslracting, imaginative elements in the same work of art shows t.li,il we have to do with a meaningfu l tendency of unconscious rc,prcscnlat,ion, and not with a "style" dependent on outward tlctcrmin,rnts. If we compare the two Benin heads in our illustrations," one, l'/.,. 18, 19 d,,spit.e its stylization, is am azingly realistic; wh ile the other, representIng a spirit, is covered with a network of symbols that produce the dis• 1,mhodying effect of tattooing. Despite the pla.sLic character of the sym· lmls, the abstracting tendency is evident. "Abstrading" representation does not, like the realistic forms of ~•·•ilptu r,,, seek to express the numinous by expressive exaggeration of 1·,·111 features, b ut by an imaginative and essentially different inner

Here we indubitably face tl1e same contradiction as in the psychology of modern man, namely, the opposition between the extraverted an<l introverted attitudes of consciousness.28 Of course it would be a fundamental error to infer a corresponding typological s tructure in the a rtist or the group. We merely note that such anti thetical psychic attitudes and modes of representation existed from the very beginning, and that they not only determine the divergent form of different objects but may also participa.te' in tlie shaping of one and the same £gure. The opposition between sensuous and imaginative recm·s in the distinction between the organic and t he geometrical or abstract in a1·t." But to call geometrical ai:t "unsympathetic" and "life-denying'' is in :, certain sense a, misunderstanding. The accentuation of a "spiritual'' dynamic, i.e., of a dynamic that moves nature but is not confined withi n nature, is not hostile to life, though it is often nonobjective. The proximity of the SJ>irit that. is independent of life to the spirits and the <lead accounts for the misunderstandi ng, but for early man the world of th<· spirits, the dead and the ancestors, though numinous, is not unsympnthetic or life-denying. Intercourse with tliese powers s tands at tlre center of man's cults and rituals precisely because, if properly approached i11 religion, art, and festivals, they pl'Omote and intensify life. iiression is characteristic of the da.ngerous negative elementary charncter of the Feminine. Consequently, we must ask what bear ing its l'rcquent absence has on the elementary character. The character of nhundance is one of man's positive, primordial experiences, a part of his experience of the Feminine as a vessel that gives nourishment. For this reason, the breasts are accentuated, while the numinous character is expressed in the dominating eyes, which together with the a.rch of the <•ycbrows ancl nose contribute strongly to the birdlike character of these ligures. •• The corporeal vessel character of t he lower body is offset hy the .spiritual eye char acter of the upper body.11 But an organ of speech, a mout.h comm unicating psychic expression, is lacking. This multiplication is perhaps most beautifully exemplified in the Peruvian jar, covered with breasts on all sides," bu t the old E uropean humped ceramics also show the archetypal breast motif. 16 Hoernes describes the technical development of these vessels as follows: "As a result of the influence of metalworking technique on pottery making, the flat circular figmes became plastic and were raised to form teat-shaped protuberances. A transitional for m is the vessel with humps, on wh ich spirnl designs were inscribed." "He s tresses the similarity of a humpecl jar from Transylvania to another such vessel from Crctc.18 Ci1·cular figure, spiral, teat, hump with spiral, hump- this sequence does not only signify a historical development for us, bu t shows in general what forms the breast motif may take on a vessel. This does not mean that wherever a circle or spiral appears on a jar the b1·eas l motiF is present, but it does indicate a connection between the hemisphere of the breast and the spiral life moti f, and suggests that the double spiral and double circle often symbolize the breasts." The archetypal situation of the mother-chi ld relationship is most 11pparent in the representations of primitive peoples. The Per uvian j,ir of the mother with child strikes us a., one of the most outstanding representations of this archetype. Here the aspect of inhumanity almost outweighs that of magnificence . The steatopygic component is unmistakable, and again U1e woman is represented as a vessel, b ut also a.s carrying a vessel and holding her breasts . Somewhere at the side of this mountainlike mass, in which the impression of fusion with the earth is intensified by the cloth falling from the head, hangs a human crealure, whose characterization lies somewhere between infant, old man, and 1Lpe. But this gigantic woman, on whom the little one "<le-pends" in the literal sense of the word , stares straight ahead of her, magnificent a nd unfeeling, monumentally embodyi ng nature's indifference towa1·d Ute living things that depend on her. Another Peruvian jar shows perhaps even more clearly the little- Pl. 41 ness of the human thing clinging to the mother. Both jars produce a numinous effect, regardless whether their prac tical purpose wa.5 sac1·ed 01· profane. In our first example of African sculpture the child is again l.iny and Pl. 39 dependent; here also the vessel stands beside the woman a;; companion Hymbol, but the expression of the woman is entirely different . It is the l.1·i11mph of a wild motherhood, whose proud, overpowering pleasure, with all its self-satisfaction, is not unrelated to Ute child. Here we do not. find the tender and personal solicitude of Isis toward her son, but l'ILI her t he royal concern of the lusty queen with that which has been liorn of her, with that which is dependent on her and nourished by her; h,•rc Ihe Feminine experiences itself-and is experienced- as mother, 11111.ure, carl.h, and giver of li fe. The Fem inine consists not only in the nurturing breast, but even Pl. 48 more in the belly-vessel that bears all things. Thus the jars in our present reproductions a re in cve,·y sense " potbellied." Some of them have at Fig. f.!O their center a circle symbolizing both navel and female genitals. Here, as in representations of the Primordial Goddess, this "generative center" bears a. symbol of life. The navel as center of the world is also archetypal. Characteristically, many shrines are looked upon as na.vcls of the world, as, for example, the Temple " at Jerusalem, the sanctuary at Delphi, and so forth. For us it is sig nificant that this symbolism unconsciously "includes" tl1e female symbolism of the earth. The earth in a sense is tlw womb of a reality seen as feminine, the navel and center from wl1ich ti"' universe is nourished.'' Cf. the shining white Parthian goddess of tlu· Pl. 49 luminous moon, who has not only gleaming eyes but a lso a radiant navel.

THE OREA'f MOTHER
be li ttle, if at all, short of improper for a man to set about making pots. The art is exclusively in the hands of women throughout North America, Central and South America, and in those parts of the :VIalay Archipelago and Peninsul a., Melanesia, and New Guinea, where the art is practiced :ts :• na.tive industry. In the Nicobar Islands pottery is made by the women only, and in the Andaman Islands it is made exclusively by the women in the northern island, while in the south island men alsv make pots. In the Pamir highlands of Central Asia the women manufacture all the pottery, and their crockery is admired for its artistic taste. In the Nilgiri Hills, among the Khotas, the pottery is made exclusively by t.he women; and the same is the case among the wild tribes of Durma. Throughout by far the greater par t of Africa pottery is made by the women only. Zulu trad ition ascribes the making of t he firsl pot to the first woman. Among certain Hamitic peoples of Uganda t.lH' industry bas, under Asiatic influence, been taken up by the men, as also in some parts of the Congo. This, however, is quite exceptional. Out of seventy-eight tribes investigated by the ethnologists attached to tlw Belgian Congo Museum, the men had no hand whatever in the makiog of pottery in sixty-seven; t he others are exceptions arising from special circum stances which in almost every instance it is easy to trace.'' "' Briffaul t cites furthe1· examples and proofs from the Con~o, Uganda, New Guinea, Tenerife, Algerii,, Tunisia, N ubia, the Pyreuc,·s , the Hebrides, Mexico, and Brazil; from among the Pygmies, the Buslo men, and others. Chapter Eleven
THE NEGATIVE ELEMENTAR Y
CH AR ACTER
B
ODY - VE SSEL and mother-child situation-the p osit ive elementary character of the Fem inine-spring from the most Intimate personal experience, from an experience that is eternally human ; and even when it is projected into the ends of heaven and earth, it preser ves its closene.ss to the central personal phenomenon of feminine li fe. The negative elementary character, however, appears in a projective ring or symbols, wb.icb do not, like those of the positive elementary charncter, spring from the visible mother-child relationship. T he negative side or the elemen tary character originates rather in inner oxperience, and the anguish, horror, and fear of danger tha t tbe Arche1.ypal Feminine signifies cannot be derived from any actual and evident ntt,·ibutes of woman. B ut since we find these negative psychic reactions HO often related to the Terrible Mother, we mus t ask, Wh at is the basis
or this primordial human fear and how is it to be interpreted? We have repeatedly called attention I to the basic psychic fact that I.he human consciousness is experienced as "masculine," and that the 1111tsculine has identified itself wi th consciousness and its growth wherc,vcr ,i patriarchal world has developed.

On the other hand, as we have shown, the unconscious, i.e., the psychic stratum from which consciousness arises in the course of huma11 history-and in the course of individual development-is experienced i11 relation to this consciousness as maternal and feminine. This does no l mean that all unconscious contents appear symbolically as feminine. Tl11· unconscious con tains masculine as well as feminine forces, tendeuci,•.s, complexes, instincts, and archetypes, just as mythology has male ai,d female gods, demons, spiriLs, animals, and so on. But in general co11sciousness sees the unconscious symbolized as feminine and itself as mascu line. The Negative Elementary Character
The most terrible of lhe three images of Kali is not the one with the inhuman many a rms, h ideously squatting amid a halo of Barnes, devouring the entrails that form a deathly umbilical cord between the corpse's open belly and her own gullet. Nor is it the one that, clad in the nocturnal black of the earth goddesses and adorned with the hacked-off hands and heads of her victims, stands on the corpse of Shiva- a barbaric specter whose exaggeration of horror makes her almost unreal. The third figme seems far more frightful because it is quieter and less barbarous . Here the hands strike us as human. One is extended, the other strokes the heads of the cobras almost as tenderly as Isis caressing the head of her ch ild; and though the phallic animal breasts l(re repellent, they recall the similar breasts of the African mother goddess. But with ils hooded head, the cobra that is twined round her waist like a girdle suggests the womb-here in its deadly aspect. This is the snake that lies coiled in the lap of the Cretan snake goddess, forms the snake robe
or the Mexican goddess Coatlicue, and girds the loins of the Greek Gorgons. And the hideous bloody tiger's tongue of the goddess is the snme as hangs down Bame-spewing between the tusks and bestia.J ijlriped breasts of the B.angda witch, or darts from between the gnashing f1mgs of the Gorgons. These figures are gruesomely alike. Their sheer frightfulness makes us hesitate, whether they represen t a skull, the head of a snake or hippopotamus, a face showing human likeness, or a head consisting of two slone knives borne by a body pieced together from p arts of snakes, pnnthers, lions, crocodiles, and human beings. So great is the inhuman, cxtrahuman, and superh uman quality in this experience of dread t hat man can visualize it only through phantoms. But all this- and it should not be forgotten- is an image not only 1 of the Feminine bu t particularly i,od specifically of the Maternal. For in ,, profound way life and birth are always bound up with death and destruction. That is why this Terrible l\liother is "Great," and this name i~ also given to Ta-urt, the gravid monster, which is hippopotamus and c:rncodilc, lioness and woman, in one. The feminine principle of the dolmen and gate is always connected with rebirth through the woman's womb. Th.is is evident from the folklore of the countries where such dolmens occur and where the sick me drnwn through them even today, and from still existing Stone Age cultures as well.1' Consequently the name for the dolmen in l.\,fa\ekula is connected with the root " to come ou t !rom, to be born." 1~ The dolmen is also a sacral house; by extension it becomes the temple and the "sacred precinct" in general. The earliest sacred precinct of the primordial age was probably that in which the woman gave birth. lt is the place where the Great Goddess rules and from which-as still in the late feminine mysteries-all males are excluded. Not only is the place of childbeariD!( the sacral place of female life in early and primitive cultu res; obviously it also stands at the <:enter of all cults that arc dedicated to the Great Goddess as the goddess of birth, fertility-and death. In l\ifalekula, for example, the name "birth enclosure" is given hoth to the fence within which the women give birth and to the one ~urroun<ling the site where the male mysteries 01 rebirth are solemnized. Thus the primitive fence 1• enclosing the female place of childbearing became a sign for the sacred precinct in general, and the process of hirth becomes a prototype for the process of rebir.th, of "higher" birth into tlic heavens as a s tar or immortal. And the symbolism of rebirth nlways goes back to the symbol ism of birth . Thus it is possible lh:~t t.l '.c "winged gate" of Sumer already meant a rebirth into heaven, wluch rs nlwa.ys symbolically related to the "winged." When in the mysteries of late antiq uity the c,indid:ite for initiation rnust take a dangerous journey through the underworld in order to uchicve rebirth, he follows the path of the sun. Thus in Apuleius,17 the lniLiate into the mysteries of Isis must pass through the twelve hours of the night, corresponding to the Egyptian conception of the underworld

In Christian myth the Devil is correlated with hell as the devouring maw of the earth; among the Aztecs he has his correspondence in Xiuhtecuhtli, the lord of fire, sitting at the center of the earth. In appearance the Christian Devil has much of the pagt1.n Pan and satyrs nbout him; his early precursor is the Egyptian Set, enemy of the soul, ndversary of Osiris and Horus. In the Book of the Dead, he appears in conjunction with the serpent Apopis as the masculine destroying aspect of the underworld. He is the slaughterer, the destroyer, the render in pieces, partner of the soul-devouring Aro-mit. He is called "The fiend, red of hair and eyes, who cometh forth by night, and doth fetter the fiend in his lair." 58 R e is the evil one, the adversary, associated with red, which is not only the positive color of fertility but also the color of calamity, evil, blood, death, and the desert, where, thousands of years Inter, the Devil appeared to tempt lhe elect. Hell and the underworld as vessels of death are forms of the negalive death-bringing belly-vessel, corresponding exactly to its life-bringing side. The opening of the vessel of doom is the womb, the gate, the gullet, which actively swallows, devours, rends, and kills. Its s uckinl( power is mythologically symbolized by its lure and attraction for n,an, !or life and consciousness and the individ ual male, who can evade it. only if he is a hero, and even then not always. This is very aptly expressed in Germanic myth and its etymological correspondences. the last days that will swallo.w up mankind .when the end of lire world npproaches and sun, moon, and stars all clash together.34 It is characteristic of the Aztec view of the world that the ni;(lo I. or doom hangs over all living things. Not only does each of the four :r;(cs or the world end with a ten-ible catastrophe, but the end of each cal..11dor division of fifty-two years is a time of doom, when the end of the worl,l is expected with terror and chastisements, and the continuation of lire is celebrated as a miracle and rebir th. This fifty-two-year interval has its conespondence in the midnight of the full day and the winter solstice of the year. At this time all vessels are destroyed; aH fire is extinguished. It is a "time of judgment" like the Jewish New Year, for example, and, os in many of the world 's New Ycar's feasts, Lhc surpassing of this d..nger point is celebrated with orgiastic rejoicing, rekind ling of the lire, and so on. The conscious world view of the Aztecs is "patriarchal"; the power
or the Goddess h as grown almost invisible, and the male principle of the light and the sun is dominant. But a closer analysis yields a very different psychological picture. Side by side with the ki ng there governs 11 figure that, though always represented by a man, bears the name of Lhc terrible Earth Mother: the "Snake Woman." She "was the executive peak of the internal affait-s of the tribe, where civil custom and religious demand governed almost every act. . . ." •1 "In Tenochtitlan the Chid of Men and the Snake Woman had double duties in respect or civil and religious affairs, the former actively leading the services and the latter Hupc.rvising the temples, the form of the rites, and the internal affairs of I.Ire priesthood." 3• Here there is no doubt that an originally matriarchal constellation w11S overlaid by patriarchal institutions. Among t he tribes of North America., we often find that the old women, without official power, l{ovcrn the internal affairs of the tribe, while the warrior chiefs govern IIr<, outward affai,·s. This corresponds to the original matriarchal situa1io11, in which the group of the women and children is governed by the
185
" Vaillant, 1'/te A.ztec.t of Mexico, p. 122, " Ibid.• p. 182.



Old Woman, while the fighting and hunting men's society is ruled by t J,,, warrior chieftain or democratically by the male group. An analysis of this Aztec constellation with its matriarchal trni l,o overlaid by patriarchal features is h ighly instructive for the history of h uman development. Originally, the men as warriors stood in the servi,·,· of the female godhead, to whom they offered blood sacrifices; and thus ii remained in the Aztec culture, although the political-social-extravert,•.t dominance had already been taken over by the male group and al though the patriarchal solar principle had-seemingly-replaced 1111• matriarclrnl lunar principle." All Aztec policies were subordinated to 1111• wars lhat were waged for the purpose of taking pfisoners to be sacriti,·,·.t in tl,c cul t of the Snake ·woman, who yielded fertility only when sa li :,tcd by tcn-ible blood sacrifices. The cruelty of the Mexican rites, which were thought to guarani<",· the fertility of the e~rth but also to reinforce masculine, solar, consciou:1 ·life, expressed the masculine consciousness' dread fear of being swal lowed up by the feminine dark aspect of the unconscious. The characteristic Aztec form of sacrifice wa.s to tear the heart 0111 of the living body and offer it up to the sun; this gave assurance of 11..fructifying rain that made the earth fertile. In another form of sacrifi,·,·, the victim was :flayed and the priest was "invested" in the skin 11 typical form of transformation symbolism. This was evidently pn· ceded by another type of sacrifice: beheading. As in Egypt, behead i111t and dismemberment originated in a surpassed matriarchal st.rat11111, vestiges of which are still discernible in the myth and rites of I lo,,
Aztecs, as, for example, in the conception of the "southern circh- ,,r hell," the circle of decapitation and dismemberment. In I\lfexico, too, the west, as its symbolism shows, is associated with the unconscious; it is the "Great Sea." And because, among the l't1exicans and the Cora Indians investigated by P reuss, the night and the night sky are also the nocturnal ocean of heaven,•0 the moon is the night snake that walks in the water,11 and the deadly western menace of the Terrible Mother is also represented as the danger of :flood. "The Cora Indians believe that in the west of the world there lives a migh ty snake, the night conceived as water, which the rising morning star" kills and the eagle, the daytime sky, devours. If I.his did not happen, the world would 611 with water." " In the westerly symbol group of the Terrible Mother-night, abyss, .,ca, watery depths, snake, dragon, whale-all the symbols color one another and merge with one another. Devouring water, rending earthwomb, abyss of death, hostile snake of night and death, whale, sea, and whale in the sea- a ll are aspects of the negative unco11scious, the "water of the depths," which lives in the nocturnal darkness beneath the world of men and threatens to £11 the world with water. Consequently, the Feminine, as the Mayan goddess Ixchel, goddess of disastrous floods and of the moon," is the fatal water jar. Her symbol is the overturned vessel of doom, on her head rests the deadly ijllake, on her hands and feet she bears sharp animal claws, and her mantle is adorned with crossed bones, t he emblem of death . The catastrophe of the flood can be averted only by the sun hero of consciousness, who over and over again defeats the serpent of the abyss. T his victory of the hero occurs in the east, where the sun rises, or, as in Mexico, at the noonday zenith,'5 and this corresponds exactly to the worship of the moon hero as new moon or full moon. symbol" of the bloody primeval Great Mother, who is herself dismembered (see above) and thereupon becomes the source of all life. Later, as hunting gave way to agriculture-regardless whether this took place before or after the conquest of Mexico by the Aztecs-the aspects changed. As an old hymn tells us, the obsidian goddess of the hunt, the obsidian butterfly, became "a goddess of the melon cactus," " and the Great Goddess changed from a goddess of the hunt, of blood and the night, to an earth goddess. The son of her fertility now became the corn, which was identical with tl1e obsidian knife-and she herself became the fertility goddess of the corn. But her old terrible nature perHisted, for fertility, death, and sacrifice belong together, and the husking of the corn is identical with the tearing out of the heart from the victim with the help of the obsidian knife. The Great Goddess bears life, the corn, which may appear as the phallic corn god or as the son of the corn, and she also bears death, the obsidian knife. This twofold aspect, in which life becomes death and 1lcath life, and in which one depends on the othe1·, recurs again and again

The explanation consists in the central content of the transformation mystery: the death of the mother of corn and earth leads to the birth of the corn, her son.70 The corn son bearing the skin of the sacrificed Earth Mother is the image of his mother's living pregnancy whereby the feminine is transformed into the masculine, which is built from the blood of the sacrificed woman. T his blood of transformation is fortified by the sacrifice. The corn-god son 71 wears a mask made from the skin of the victim's thighs, and this is a repetition of the same symbolism of transformation and birth 01· rebirth. The identity of mother and son in this fertility mystery is intensi• fied by the fact that before being sacrificed the woman representing the corn goddess wears the mantle of maguey " characteristic of the cornson. The principal ceremony of the flaying festival is the sword-fight sacrifice, a battle between selected prisoners equipped with sham weapons and fully armed warriors-ending, of course, in the death and sacrifice of the prisoners. The prisoners whose death in battle forms the contents of the sword fight stand on a large stone some ten feet in diameter. They arc bound with a rope, the "foodstuff rope," which starts at the ccnl.c1· of the stone "representing the center and entrance of the cnrt.1,- iu lite sexual as in other respects," and which has the evident cltaracl.cr of the umbilical cord.'3 The prisoner who is weary or simply unaL,lc to fight is kmed in a position that Danzel designates as the receiving position, but it might equally well be characterized M a position of birth. In tl1is connection, it should be mentioned that a mother who has given birth to a child is said to have "made a prisoner," and that a woman who dies in childbirth is called a "sacrificed prisoner." Thus it becomes understandable that the warrior sacrificed on Ihe round stone should also be regarded as Huitzilopochtli.•• His symbol is the hummingbird, "symbol of the vegetation of the beginning of I.he rainy season." ""As a spirit of fertility, the god bears a leg of this hummingbird, which is associated with the maize tree in the west, the realm of the Great l\1:ot11er.101 He is a god of the sun and of war; he is born of the virgin mother, who is impregnated by a shuttlecock from heaven, a variant of the motif of "supernatural birth" or fecundation by the sun phallus as source of the wind.1•• As son of the snake-mantled mother, he is entwined in snakes; his scepter is a snake; his drum is a snakeskin.1"' As in the sun phallus, the relationship of sun, snake, and wind recurs in the figure of Huitzilopochtli. He symbolizes tbe generative luminous aspect, which is also the dominant element in the other hero god of the Aztecs, Quetzalcoatl, the plumed serpent.1"' As war god, Huit.zilopochtli was also the fecundator, for to wage war meant to take prisoners, whose blood was essential to the fertility both of the gods and the world. The husking of the corn, the heart, is castration, mutilation, and Mcrifice of the essential male part; but at the same time it is birth and n lire-giving deed for the benefit of the world or mankind. It is the death nnd downfall of the earthly principle; but by virtue of its transpersonal relation to the gods and the powers, with which it is identical as sun nnd godhead, this earthly principle is uroborically whole and immortal nnd " lives" in the land of the sun, i.e., in t he luminous world of the east. The clima.., of this development occurs- as in Malekula and in Egypt-in the sacral formula "I and the Father are one," by which the individual emancipates himself from the domination of the Great Mother. The progress to the male consciousness and the autonomy of the

Budge sums up her significance in words tha.t describe the Great Goddess in her full scope. "The statements of Greek writers, taken together with the evidence deri ved from the hieroglyphic texts, prove that in very early times Net was the personification of the eternal female principle of life which was self-sustaining and self-existent and was secret nod unknown and all-pervading; the more material thinkers, whilst admitting that she brought forth her son Ra without the a id of a husband, were unable to divorce from their minds the idea that a male germ was necessary for this production, and finding it impossible to derive it rrom a power or being external to the goddess, assumed that she herself provided not only the substance which was to form the body of Ra but also the male germ which fecundated it. Thus Net wa.s the prototype of parthenogenesis.'' THE CREAT MOTHEU.
THE GODDESS OF FATE
After all that has been said, it is not hard to see that space is o,w of the most important projections of the Feminine as a totality; if there were no other indications of this, it would follow from her char acter of containing vessel and cosmic egg. But the Feminine is alsu the goddess of time, and thus of fate. The symbol in which spa,·,· and time arc archctypally connected is the starry firniament, whid, since the primorrlial era has been filled with human projections. Hen· ii is a matter of indifference whethei -as in Egypt, Babylonia, Arahi:i. India, China, and America- the heavens are interpreted as originally. according to the primordial twenty-eight stations of the moon, or a•. later, accord ing to the twel ve s tations of the sun; and whether animal 111· plant, stream or ocean , are t he dominant projections.' The essent i:i.l point is that each one of these projections was experienced as a put of the We of the Great Goddess, who bears and encompasses all things. The dependency of all the luminous bodies, of all the heavcu l,_. powers and gods, on the Great Mother, their rise and fall, their birlI, and death, tlieir transformation and renewal, are among the roost pro found experiences of mankind . Not only the alternation of day and nii-,:l,1 but also the changes of the months, seasons, and years are subordinat.-.1 to the all-powerful will of the Great iVIother. And that is why, not 0111.v as the Mesopotamfan Tiaroat, but throughout the world, she holds 11,.. tables of fate, the all-determining constellations of heaven, which is lwr self. And accordingly the Great Mother, adorned with the moon and I I,,. starry cloak of night, is the goddess of destiny, weaving life a,; s li,· weaves fate. Since she governs growth, the Great Mother is goddess of t.i1111•, That is why she is a moon goddess, for moon and night sky arc 11,.. visible manifestations of the temporal process in the cosmos, and 11,.. moon, not the sun, is the true chronometer of the primordial era. Fn,,., menstruation, with its supposed relation to the moon, pregnancy, :011.I beyond, the woman is regulated by and dependent on time; so iI ;,.

Tlw Great Round: 'l'he Goddess of Fate
she who determines time-to a far greater extent than the 111ale, wilIi bis tendency toward the conquest of time, toward ti rncl c.ss111;ss «11d eternity.• And this temporal quality of the Feminine is bound up wi llo the moon. The temporal quality as well as the element of water arc 1.n 111· correlated with t he Feminine, and this connection is made plai,1 in the symbol "stream of time." T he primordial mystery • of weaving and spinnin5 has also been ei--perienced in projection upon the Great l\fotber who weaves the web of life and spi ns the threads of fate, regardless whether she appears as one Great Spinstress or, as so frequently, in a lunar triad . It is not by accident that we speak of the body's "tissues," for the tissue woven by the Feminine in the cosmos and in the uterus of woman is life and destiny. And astrology, the study of a destiny governed by the stars, teaches that both begin at once, at the temporal moment of birth. Thus the Great Goddesses are weavers, in Egypt as in Greece, among the Germanic peoples and the Mayans. And because "reality" is wrought' by the Great Weavers, all such activities as pla.itiog, weaving, and knotting belong to the fate-governing activity of the woman, who, as Bachofen discovered,' is a spinner and weaver in her natural
aspect. The crossing of the threads is the symbol of sexual union- we still speak of a crossing of animals and plants-and the crossing or I.he sexes is the basic form in which the Archetypal Feminine "weaves" lire.• Thus the Great Round is the world-containing and world -creating uterus, in which the real; the corporeal, and the material are formed by the Great Mother herself. The temporal moment of our birth (the epoch) and the body (the constitution) are the fated components of the individual existence, which everyone who seeks a fundamental understanding of the contingency of the individual life must accept.

Round is a bronze Etruscan lamp,35 in the centel' of which lhc Gorgon's head is surrounded by an intricate wreath of figures, Leiscgang has giv,·n us a penetrating interpretation of this piece.'t T he gOTgoneion is I lw mid night sun of the underworld- the teniblc face of the Great Mol.11cr. A,·ound this center lies a first circle, on which a &'1'iffin and a lion each rend to pieces an animal that is mortal life predestined to death. T he second circle, the watery region with its wreath of twenty-eight waves (Lhe lunar number), is inhabited by eight dolphins. The number eight forms the transition to the region of air and fire, dominated by eight sirens with birds' wings and ln,man faces, "squatting on a three-stepped l,nse, covered with fine lines that I should interpret as rain falling from lhe clouds of the air. Beside each sil'en squats a flute-playing silenus with erect phallus; he symbol izes the rain that falls from heaven to rcrtilize the earth.. . . Over the head of each of the sixteen figures we sec a sun and ten stars, indicating the stellar realm, which extends to the real fire and light of the sixteen flames on the upper side of the bowl, directly over the heads of the sirens and sileni, presum ably representing Lhe luminous supl'acclestial realm to which the soul attains when it has passed t he outer most sphe1·e of heaven." 37 Thus the lamp represents the ascend ing str ucture of the world, whose base and center is the gorgoneion. In the Edda, F rigg and Gefjou foresee the destinies of the wo!'ld, Groa sings magic songs over Thor, and Odin takes his wisdom rrom the volvas. In the sagas, the seeresses are highly honored in their wanderings among the peasants, and there is even confirmation of the statement that 'some were exalted to the rank of goddesses' (Tacitus, [Ji.~lmy, IV, 61), for Jar! Haakon ordered a special temple to be built for the seeress Thorgerd. "However, not only the seeress, but the element itself can become a mediating voice between man and the primordial decree oi fate (and this is an indication that the woman comes to her high calling not as a person but as a representative of the universal). Everything that dwells in the depths, close to the Norns, is fraught with destiny, and most of all the water that rises up from the depths and the tree rooted in them. Water and tree are for this reason the roost important elementary symbols, and they were endowed with a primordial sacredness by all Germanic tribes." ,. In another place Ninck writes : "Odin's ,·elation to Mimir reflects his relation to the primordially sacred water of destiny, and t.he myth of his banging from Yggdrasill reflects his relation to the tree of fat,·. So close was his bond with the spring that Odin here set up his judgrn('nl. seat, and the name of the world ash tree points to the gnmt signi fican,·,· attached to the hanging myth; for Yggdrasill is the 'steed of llggr, of him who frightened Odin.' The importance of this m,vt I, follows also rrom the previously mentioned sacrifices by hanging, offered up Lo the god in connection with the act of initiation performed by himself, and of the scaldic names for Odin: Hangaguj,, 'Hanging God'; Geigujir, 'the Dangling One'; Galgagramr, 'Lord of the Gallows'; and Hangi, 'the Jfanged One.' Scarcely any aspect of their religion so facilitated the conversion of the Germans to Christianity as the apparent similarity of their hanged god to the crucified Christ, and here it is characteristic l.h:it the Goths and Anglo-Sa.xons rendered the word 'cross' in their language as 'gallows' (Goth. galgc1, OE . geolga) . The L,1dy ~f the Plants
Since the investigations of Mannhardt and Frazer, the central role of the Maypole and Christmas tree in vegetation rites has been so well known that we need merely mention it in passing. The numinous-feminine character of the tree speaks to us in the manticism not only of Greece and the Germanic countries but also of. the Old Testament.•• We kn ow of the veneration in which the tree was held among the Semites - the t ree cult on the heights; the worship of the cull pole or Asherah, the goddess of heaven; and the ritual dance around the trcc--from the polemics against it in the Old Testament. The tree, however, has not only feminine characteristics; in accordance with its uroboric nature it has mascul ine ones as well. T he "spirit" of the tree is not necessarily or a. female, dryad type; there ma.y be a male principle, the serpent, dwelling in its branches, as, for example, in the myth of l\1edea or of Adam an<l Eve. The switch, wand, and bough, phallic symbols with which the Female is beaten in certain fertil ity rituals, are the most familiar expression of these relationships. We have elsewhere dealt at length with lhe youthful lover as plant and
tree. " Bu t in his character of tree and tendril , the male is "contained"; he ret ains his dependency on the feminine ear th-womb character. Though the "family tree" often takes the form of a phallus, the c:crth in which this masculinity is rooted, and which lives in the dep ths hehind the phallic male principle, is the Great Goddess. Bachofen quotes the solemn oath of the women of l'ricnc- "In the darkness of the oak . . ."-and comments: "The women invoke I.he primordial mother of dark matter, not her product that has shot up into the light, the nocturnal oak. Higher than the tree of the gods stands the primordial shadow, the dark womb whence the tree sprang, Io which the dead retu rn, and which the women therefore invoke in lhci,· most ardent oath."" In this aspect the tree belongs to that stratum of life and growth which is most directly attached to the earth. Older than this stratum is only that of the sacred stones and mountains,

This "division of labor" among early mankind is always archetypally conditioned and c«11not be explained " from outside," i.e., sociologically.64 There is no such thing as a sexual fitness or un.fitncss for this or that task. \~1c find inactive mc11 and wan·ior women just as we find inactive women and warrior men, and the relation of the group to the powers may be the affair eitlier of men or of women. For t.hc life of tl1c group, the " psychic means of productjon" arc at least of equal iroport.mcc wi th the economic. The Lady of the Plants
The "virginity" of the Great Mother, i.e., her independence of the male, becomes particularly evident in the Amaronian bee state, where only the queen is fecundated by the male, and she only once. For this 1·cason, and because of the food she cats, the bee is pure; Demeter is the "pure mother bee," ,. and the bee priestesses of Demeter must, like tJ:ie Vestals and many other priestesses of the Great ]Vfother, be virgins. And nmong the bees, as so often among beasts and men, matriarchal womanhood ~ssumcs a character of the "terrible" in its relation to the males; for after mating, the drone mate and all other drones are sluin like aliens by the female group inhabiting the hive. The beehive is an attribute of the Great Goddess ns DcmctcrCcres-Spes. But the bee is also associated with the moon: the priestesses Fig. 58 of the moon goddess were called "bees,'' and it was believed that all honey came from the moon, the hive whose bees were the stars." Honey is the vital essence, the supreme nutriment of the plant mother, b ut it also has its death symbolism. Bachofcn pointed to the connection between Glaucus' burial in honey and the matriarchal principle of Lycia, and this theme has been developed by a recent writer.;, The vessel of death with its maternally noutishing honey is the instrument of a honeyed death, embalmment in honey. " 'To fall into a jar of honey' is to be identified with 'to die.' In the early situation of human cultlll'e, the group psyche was dominant. What made this phenomenon possible wu.s that the differences between man, animal, plant, and the inorganic world were not perceived as in modern consciousness. Lulia n origin); and as the Hallstatt goddess on the bronze hydria-as the (:mat Artemis and Diana-she is queen of the animal world. ·what interests us here is not the forms assumed by this Great (:oddess in the different parts or the worid. Her names are innumerable - Britomartis and Dictynna, Cybele and Ma, Dindymeoe and Hecate, l'heraia and Artemis, Baubo and Aphaia, Orthia and Nemesis, Demeter, Persephone, and Selene, Medusa and Eleuthera, Taeit and Leto, Aphrodite and Bendis. And Hathor and Isis, and all other Great Goddesses who appear in animal form, are in reality the Lady of the Beasts. All beasts are their subjects: the serpent and scorpion,.the fishes of river nn<l sea, the womblike bivalves and the ill-omened kraken, the wild hcasts of wood and mountain, hunting and hunted, peaceful and voracious, the swamp birds-goose, duck, and heron-the nocturnal owl and the dove, the domesticated beasts-cow and bull, goat, pig, 1tll(l sheep- the bee, and even such phantasms as griffin and sphinx." In naming all these, we are practicing an age-old rite. Such lists 11~ we find in Apuleius, who identifies Isis with innumerable kindred f!Oddesses,18 are a form of ritual worship. T he abundance of manifcsta1ions is a characteristic of the archetype, and the plethora of names by which the powers are invoked among all peoples is an expression of their uuminous ineffability . As our illustrations show, the Lady of the Beasts is o£1.cn wini-:cd; Il11tt is to say, she is a heavenly and not a chl.honi<: godde.ss. THR WOMAN AS MANA FIGURE
'l'lius there unfolds before us a magnificent world of feminine cultural ,t.•vclopment, wh ich is at the same time an unfolding of feminine power. A sil ver vessel of this kind, the so-called Gundestrap caldron, with Pl. 140 a.sacrifici al scene represented on it, has been found in Jutland . The goddess depicted on it , whom Briffault describes as a moon goddess,8 is already known to us a..5 the Lady of the Beasts. In the present reproductions two types are clearly differentiated : first, the old priestesses, whose hair is worn like that of the Great Goddess of the Beasts, toward whom they turn; these evidently are the actual priestesses of fertility and prophecy. Beside them we see two "hovering" young female figures resembling the young witches who later rode on broomsticks. They are Artemislike, Amazonian goddesses of the hunt. One battles with a wild beast; the other appears to he slaying an aurochs . We shall have more to say of the relation between the young and old priestess or deity. The magic caldron is 01·iginally a symbol of fertility belonging to Lhe elementary character of the Feminine. As such it yields food , it is the cornucopia, for example; and even its latest form, the Chris Iian, sublimitted Grail, which has almost lost its original significance as the magical kettle of the cult priestess, retains its food-giv ing aspc<:l.. ERICH NEUl\!IANN
Uurn in Berlin in 1905, Erich Neumann earned his P h .D. :d: I.Ii~ UniYursity of Berlin in 10'27. He then began medic,,! studies in l!c,rl in and 011111pleted the examinations for the degl'ee in 1933, after which he le-It llcmnany. He studied with C. G. Jung in 1934 and 1936, and from l !)!l-1 bl~ pe1·manent home was Tel Aviv, where he practiced as an analytic:il JIN,vchologist. For many years he returned regularly to Zurich to lecture IL the C. G. Jung Institute. From 1948 until 1960 he was a regular tn11Lributor at the Eranos meeti111,,s in Ascona, Switzerland, and he also l1c1l,11 red frequently in England, France, and the Netherlands. He was a
n1c-111 ber of the International Association for Analytical Psychology «nd
l
ll'<'si<lent of the Israel Association of Analytical l'sychologists. He died II 'l'cl Aviv on November 5, 1960. Dr. Neumann had a theoretical and phil osophical approach to ann lysis contrasting with the more clinical concern in E ngland and the Un ited States. His most valuable contribution to psychological theory IN Lhe empirical concept of "ccntrnversion," a synthesis of extra- and lnl,roversion. His philosophical considerations of psychology are conlnlucd io Tiefenpsyclwlogie und n1me Ethih (1949), but he is best known lor his statements of a coherent theory of feminine development. In 7'/w Origins and History of Consciowness (1949; tr. 1954), which illustr11l.cs by interpretations of basic mythologems the a rchetypal stages in 1,11<' ,lcvelopment of human consciousness, the emphasis on matri;irchal •,y111holism fol'eshadowed his monumental work The Great Mother (firsL p11 hl ished in English, 1955), a study of the :i\iagna Mater in the arl of
All Limes and in ethnological and mythological documents. Other works dcm ling with the idea of the feminine are Amor ancl Ps71che: The Psyeldc / )n11,:Lo1nnent of the Feminine-A Commenlar'IJ on the Tale /:,y Apuu:ius (IIW2; tr. 1956) and The Archetypal World of Ilenry Moore (first publM11'd in English, 1959). The extent of his interests and his penetrating Qc1111prchens ion of the fine arts arc demonstrated by essays on Leonardo
cln Vinci, 11:arc Chagall, l\1ozart, ICaika, Georg Tra.kl, Jewish symbol ism, and many other topics. Some or these are contained in his colli11•kcl cs.says ( Umlcrei.nin(J der 1'11.itte, 3 vols., 1953--54), four of which ftn, Lra11slnted in his Art and the Creative Unconscious (1959).
